#### Pauses in US-Russian Arctic cooperation increases trust & scientific knowledge gaps between scientists necessary for successful projects. The longer we wait the more insurmountable these gaps become 

**Williams 2025**. Margaret Williams, Senior Fellow, Arctic Initiative,
Belfer Center for Science and International Affairs \| Harvard Kennedy
School. "U.S.-Russian Cooperation on Science and Conservation in the
Bering Strait Region: Past Achievements and Current Status" Belfer
Center Report. June 2025.
\<https://www.belfercenter.org/sites/default/files/2025-06/Williams_Bering%20Strait%20Interviews.pdf\>
Accessed 6/29/25. ARJSH

1\. Knowledge Gaps [[A key area of bilateral work between Russia and
Alaska has been assessing the impacts of rising Arctic air temperatures
on permafrost]{.mark}, the layer of frozen soils which stretches across
much of the circumpolar. Russia encompasses most of the
permafrost-covered soils in the Arctic.]{.underline} The wartime
prohibition on communication meant that [U.S. scientists stopped sending
equipment]{.underline} to Siberia, [and Russian scientists could no
longer send data to their Western partners]{.underline}. One Russian
permafrost scientist noted that in his field, [an interruption in shared
data collection and analysis can be tolerated for a short
while]{.underline} because anticipated changes in Siberia can be
inferred by research in Scandinavia and Alaska. However, the scientist
said that, [[in the long term, the loss of information coming from
Russia will cause "estimates]{.mark} of changes in global permafrost
\[[to be\] biased]{.mark}."]{.underline} 2. Decline in Trust [Russian
and American interviewees alike spoke about [the value of]{.mark} both
personal and professional relationships with their respective
international colleagues]{.underline}. They recounted how [[developing
research questions jointly,]{.mark} then working together[, often for
multiple years in a row, built strong foundations of trust and
respect]{.mark} for the other "side." These bonds helped many
cooperative projects flourish over three decades. [This core element of
successful bilateral work]{.mark},]{.underline} however, [[is now in
jeopardy, as two years of]{.mark} little to [no]{.mark} in-person
[fieldwork (during the pandemic), followed by three years of]{.mark}
virtually [no communication, have eliminated the opportunities for
Russian and American experts to]{.mark} meet, share ideas, conduct
fieldwork, and [nurture]{.mark} their [working
relationships]{.mark}]{.underline}. [Two other factors may be expected
to dilute the trust that developed between American and Russian
biologists and experts over the past 30 years. First, anti-American
propaganda has become deeply entrenched in Russian society, particularly
in the media, since Russia invaded Ukraine. Second, the Trump
administration is hollowing out federal natural resource management
agencies and scientific organizations, further decreasing the likelihood
that people once actively involved in bilateral projects will be able to
reignite their relationships.]{.underline} Conclusion & Recommendations
A lot was accomplished to understand, protect conserve the shared
environment when scientists and indigenous hunters/observers worked
together and the borders were porous. Today, climate change and
industrial activities are transforming the Bering Strait region. To
understand, manage, and adapt to those changes, it is essential to have
a picture of what is happening on both sides of the strait. As the
United States' relationship with Russia takes a new turn, there may be
opportunities to renew some forms of communication and cooperation at
the working level.

#### Arctic ecosystem collapse spills over globally as southern species begin to fill in crowding out native populations. Now is key to act

**Guynup 2024**. Sharon Guynup reports on wildlife and environmental
issues for U.S. and international publications "The new Arctic: Amid
record heat, ecosystems morph and wildlife struggle" Mongabay February
14, 2024.
\<https://news.mongabay.com/2024/02/the-new-arctic-amid-record-heat-ecosystems-morph-and-wildlife-struggle/\>
Accessed 6/26/25. ARJSH

A changing menu, an altered food chain [Every ecosystem forms an
intricate weave of life that evolved over millennia. [In the harsh polar
environment, a few staple foods]{.mark} drive the system[,
includ]{.mark}ing [Arctic plankton and zooplankton]{.mark} (copepods)
that surround near-shore ice and feed even the largest residents:
filter-feeding bowhead whales. [These microscopic
species]{.mark}]{.underline} (Falk likens them to "fast food"), [offer
oil-rich, high-calorie sustenance, and provide sufficient energy to
survive the cold. In the warming northern Atlantic]{.underline}
[Ocean]{.underline} (which logged record-high temperatures in 2023),
[native phytoplankton and copepod species are dwindling. They are slowly
being supplanted by smaller, less nutritious southern species that are
shifting their ranges northward.]{.underline} Clement emphasizes [[the
potential chain reaction of Arctic planktonic species becoming less
available]{.mark}: "A shift in the nutritional or energetic capacity at
the bottom [has knock-on effects all the way up the food web,"]{.mark}
he says. The dovekie]{.underline} (a.k.a. little auk), [a seabird that
feeds mostly on zooplankton crustaceans, is a keystone species of the
Arctic ecosystem]{.underline}. These black-and-white robin-sized birds
are the far north's unlikely farmers. [Guano and eggshells from their
massive hillside nesting colonies fertilize vegetation on the land
below. Those plants then feed everything from birds and lemmings to
hares and reindeer. Top predators --- Arctic foxes, gyrfalcons and polar
bears --- then prey on the nesters, grazers and birds, including
dovekies.]{.underline} But within the dovekie's range, novel laser
technology has shown [that copepod populations are shifting from
calorie-rich Arctic species to less nourishing Atlantic varieties. [Due
to these declining and changing food sources]{.mark}, Falk notes that
dovekie parents must now fly farther, dive deeper or bring back more
food to nourish their young]{.underline}. Chicks often fledge at lower
weight, threatening their survival during the following winter. If this
trend continues over the long term, the fallout could cascade across the
tundra. [[Many animals]{.mark},]{.underline} including black guillemots
and harp seals, [[are]{.mark} also [being forced to spend more energy to
eat and raise their offspring]{.mark}. Polar cod are another critical
link in the food chain. These rich, oily pelagic fish thrive in subzero
temperatures, spawn under the ice, grow extremely fast, and sustain a
multitude of animals. They're being supplanted by larger Atlantic cod
expanding northward, which like southern copepods, aren't as
calorie-rich as Arctic species. It's not apples-to-apples when it comes
to nutrition]{.underline}, says Rolf Anker Ims, a professor of Arctic
and marine biology at the Arctic University of Norway. "Ice-linked
species are better meals." Subarctic invaders and the Arctic squeeze
[Zooplankton and Atlantic cod shifts are just part of a larger
phenomenon. The boundary between subarctic and Arctic ecosystems is
being rejiggered by the year, prompting an influx of invaders from the
south, both in the ocean and on land. Newcomers]{.underline}, like red
fox, moose, snowshoe hare and other opportunist boreal mammals, [are
starting to outcompete or push out longtime residents]{.underline}.
Grizzly bears moving northward are now preying on Arctic-dwelling
muskox. [Invasive North American beavers have constructed some 12,000
ponds in western Alaska, [altering the availability of water and melting
permafrost]{.mark}. Southern seabirds are increasingly present,
including albatrosses that soar over the Bering and Chukchi seas,
competing for food with resident and migratory birds that have long
nested there. Plant life is changing, and on the move, too]{.underline}.
"The tundra is much greener than it was 30 years ago," says Ims. [Shrubs
and trees moving up-latitude are edging out the mosses and lichens that
provide winter food for many species. [Even the largest marine mammals
are under threat from invaders]{.mark}. Iced-in areas were once safe
havens for bowhead whales, belugas and narwhals (the unicorns of the
sea), protecting them from killer whales that now foray into ice-free
Arctic waters]{.underline}. "[They don't have defenses against
pack-hunting orcas]{.underline}," says Clement. "[It's going to change a
lot of predator-prey relationships in the marine environment." There's
even speculation that orcas could unseat polar bears as the region's top
predator.]{.underline} Some resident species are responding to climate
change by moving up, like snowy owls, which are nesting farther north in
Siberia. A large majority of the Arctic's 200 breeding bird species are
migratory, arriving from around the world; they, too, are altering their
habits. One dramatic example is the sparrow-like Lapland longspur. Back
in 2017, researchers discovered the birds were nesting more than 640
kilometers (400 miles) north of their previous east Greenland range.
However, "there's a limit to how far they can go," says Falk, creating
an "Arctic squeeze" where animals run out of room. "Habitat on the
coldest parts of the planet is limited and continues to shrink," he
says, which could intensify Arctic competition as animals jostle for
position in an existential game of musical chairs. [An uncertain future
with nowhere to go]{.underline} For Arctic animals, [seasonal cues are
critical to survival, signaling when to migrate, mate, and where to hunt
and forage. But field researchers are increasingly documenting
[circumpolar changes]{.mark} that are shifting animals' life events by
days, weeks or months.]{.underline} Biologist Kyle Joly has observed
dramatic timing changes in migrating caribou in northern Alaska. He's
tracked them since 2009, capturing the animals from boats as they swam
the Kobuk River and fitting them with GPS collars. At first, he and his
U.S. National Park Service colleagues collared the animals in early
September. Just a decade later, the animals didn't begin crossing until
the start of November, a full two months later. In many cases, such
[shifts [can create a widening divergence between a species and its food
supply]{.mark} --- a timing mismatch that can prove deadly.]{.underline}
But [climate change isn't the only human-caused Arctic disruption[. The
top of the world is a hotspot for expanding industries that could
devastate ecosystems]{.mark}, including oil and gas exploration, marine
mining, commercial fishing, as well as trans-Arctic shipping and booming
tourism. The polar region is also a repository for chemicals and heavy
metals arriving from the developed world, riding on wind and ocean
currents to pollute the pole: microplastics, pesticides, "forever
chemicals,"]{.underline} PFAS, PCBs, mercury, and other persistent toxic
substances. [Many contaminants have serious health impacts, disrupting
brain function, causing cancer, and interfering with
reproduction.]{.underline} In the long term, [some Arctic species will
succeed better than others.]{.underline} Rolf Ims notes that "[winners"
in this ecosystem shuffle will likely be generalist species that are
able to cope with a wider climatic niche, colonize new areas, or adjust
to new circumstances.]{.underline} But, as climate change and other
environmental problems escalate, [[immense uncertainty remains even for
those animals currently finding success in a rapidly rearranging
region]{.underline}]{.mark}. While many birds can fly somewhere else,
[many species can't move. "[We've seen massive die-offs from starvation
or disease]{.mark},"]{.underline} says Clement. There are open questions
regarding how polar bears will adapt to more time on land and greater
competition with range-shifting grizzlies. And what [about those species
that can't, or won't, move, like sea ice-linked narwhals, belugas and
ivory gulls, as well as sea-floor creatures or thick-billed murres,
which are loyal to their breeding sites?]{.underline} No one knows what
the threats will look like, but a key takeaway, Clement says, [is that
the rate of change is accelerating. "[Species can adapt over time, but
they don't have time, and ecosystems are really complicated]{.mark}. I'm
not clear which species will prevail and where]{.underline}." Falk fears
that "[[we are losing whole systems, whole communities of plants and
animals]{.mark} ... that will be replaced by something else. It's
impossible to project 30, 40, 50 years into the future, but it's really,
really frightening]{.underline}," he says. Clement's ultimate message:
"We must quickly stop burning fossil fuels, the main culprit in climate
change." But the world isn't making that kind of progress: In 2023
global carbon emissions from oil, gas and coal rose by 1%, reaching a
record high, according to the nonprofit Global Carbon Project.
["[Everything we do now reduces suffering in the
future]{.mark}]{.underline}," says Clement.

#### Biodiversity loss causes extinction

Emile **Torres**, Scholar at the Institute for Ethics and Emerging
Technologies, 5-20-20**16**, \"Biodiversity Loss: An Existential Risk
Comparable to Climate Change,\" Future of Life Institute,
https://futureoflife.org/2016/05/20/biodiversity-loss/

**[Catastrophic consequences for civilization]{.underline}**. The
[consequences of this rapid pruning]{.underline} [of the evolutionary

cooperation but does not require it, nor provide a comprehensive plan to
achieving it. The United States should seek to achieve further
collaboration with Russia on SAR coordination through enhanced
information sharing between all entities involved in SAR operations.

Infrastructure development in the Arctic could also help SAR

other Arctic nations to increase investment in infrastructure.

**Svoboda 24** \[Jan Svoboda (AMO Research Fellow and Project
Coordinator), 1-30-2024, "China's Emission Trading System Leaves a Lot
to Desire," China Observers in Central and Eastern Europe,
https://chinaobservers.eu/chinas-emission-trading-system-leaves-a-lot-to-desire/,
njn; accessed 6-27-2025\]

[[China needs to **step up**]{.mark} ambitions on [its **emission
trading**]{.mark} **system** [to contribute to]{.mark} global
**[decarbonization]{.mark}** efforts]{.underline}.

China profiles itself as a country with an ambitious climate and
environmental policy. In 2020, President Xi Jinping announced his
intention to make China a carbon-neutral country by 2060, while adding
that China's total emissions should reach an absolute peak before 2030.

After seven pilot projects in Chinese cities and provinces, in 2021,
China launched a national carbon market, or emissions trading system
(ETS) which immediately became the world's largest in terms of covered
emissions. While the short-term goal of China's ETS is to raise
awareness around emissions monitoring and to help businesses realize
emitting carbon is linked with a cost, in the long term, the objective
of the scheme is to provide emitting companies with a market-driven
incentive to reduce emissions from the emission-intensive electricity
sector. In the EU, the European Emissions Trading System (EU ETS) has
helped reduce emissions from power plants and industry by 37 percent
since 2005.

The goal of climate neutrality and the plan to reach the emissions peak
by 2030 certainly surprised many, also because China has historically
never been a pioneer of climate policies. China is the world's largest
producer of greenhouse gasses -- it accounts for about 26 percent of
global emissions, and therefore, from the point of view of limiting
global warming, it is very important if and how it meets its emission
obligations. Can China's ETS play the same role in reducing emissions of
the country's energy and industrial sectors as its European predecessor
did in the EU?

A Broken Market?

China's ETS includes more than 2,000 companies which produce about 4.5
billion tons of carbon dioxide annually. For comparison, in 2021, the EU
ETS had a limit of 1.6 billion tons of CO2. While the European system
covers power plants and heating plants, industrial production plants
such as refineries, cement plants, steel plants, as well as
intra-European flights and maritime transport, the Chinese carbon market
covers only electricity production. At the same time, already in 2016,
when the launch of the system was announced, China proposed covering
other sectors such as iron and steel production, aluminum, cement and
the paper industry, i.e. the most emission-intensive sectors of the
economy.

However, [[the carbon market has **not**]{.mark} **yet
[expanded]{.mark}** [to **other industries**]{.mark}, mainly due to
insufficient quality and a general lack of data]{.underline}. While the
exact date of expansion is unknown, the Ministry of Ecology and the
Environment is currently counting on the inclusion of these sectors in
the emission trading scheme in 2025. But it could happen even earlier,
as the recent news about the inclusion of the cement and aluminum
sectors indicates. [This is because **sectoral** expansion is becoming a
**priority** due to **insufficient** market **liquidity**.]{.underline}
[[**More market participants** would lead to a **higher-quality price
signal** which would result in **more market
activity**.]{.underline}]{.mark}

However, insufficient coverage is not the main problem of China's carbon
market. [[The **EU**]{.underline}]{.mark} ETS is a so-called
cap-and-trade system. The [[permit market has a set **emission
ceiling**]{.mark} for the total amount of **greenhouse gasses** that
facilities on the carbon market can **emit**. [This]{.mark}
ceiling]{.underline} is expressed in emission allowances, and it
[[**decreases** every year]{.underline}]{.mark}. Companies included in
the system must hand over allowances for each ton of CO2 emitted under
the threat of heavy fines. At the same time, they trade allowances with
each other as needed.

Within the framework of pilot projects, even before the launch of the
national carbon market, China cooperated closely with the European
Commission and its experts on the topic of carbon markets. However, the
two schemes are far from being similar. [[China's ETS has **no**]{.mark}
**absolute [ceiling]{.mark}** [and this is]{.mark} also [the **biggest
difference**]{.mark} between the two]{.underline} emission trading
systems. Therefore, [the Chinese ETS is **not even** a cap-and-trade
system yet]{.underline}. What is more, emission allowances are allocated
free of charge in the Chinese system, depending on how much electricity
a given power plant produces. The system of free allocation also
persists within the EU carbon market. Every year, however, the amount of
allocated allowances for the manufacturing industry decreases. In
principle, European power plants have not received any allowances for
free since 2013, which also led to a significant reduction in emissions
from the energy sector.

[[Emission allowances are]{.mark} therefore [**cheap** in
China]{.mark}]{.underline} -- one currently costs around 60 RMB, or â‚¬7
to â‚¬8 (about ten times less than the current price of the EU's emission
allowance). This is a similar price to the European carbon market
between 2012 and 2017, when the system also struggled to reallocate
allowances for free. Clearly, [[the design]{.mark} of a carbon market
[has **major impacts** on]{.mark} the resulting allowance price and thus
on [the system's]{.mark} overall **[success]{.mark}**]{.underline} in
reducing emissions in the covered sectors. [If China's ETS is [to be
**successful**]{.mark} and an example of an effective policy to reduce
greenhouse gas emissions, [it **must** undergo]{.mark} a **[major
reform]{.mark}**]{.underline}.

What incentive does China's carbon market offer to reduce emissions when
its allowances are allocated according to the number of verified
emissions? Those companies that are able to produce less emissions per
unit of electricity produced can generate a surplus of allowances for
sale -- this is how the allowance price is formed as well. At the same
time, China has an incentive to exempt its companies from paying carbon
border fees.

**That's causing [record-setting]{.underline} pollution.**

**Huang 24** \[Yanzhong Huang (Senior fellow for global health, Council

That said, [[it is **premature** to celebrate the **end** of

Î¼g/mÂ³ [[still **falls short** of the **WHO

Î¼g/mÂ³. To put this into perspective, the most polluted city in North

pollutants]{.underline}, such as ozone (Oâ‚ƒ). Surface ozone is a
significant air pollutant that exacerbates respiratory conditions like
asthma and chronic obstructive pulmonary disease, and increases the risk
of infections and cardiovascular diseases. In the absence of a
multi-pollutant emission reduction strategy that coordinates the control
of both PM2.5 and Oâ‚ƒ pollution, the density of O3 tends to rise as PM2.5
concentrations fall. Prior to the pandemic, there had been a significant
increase in O3 concentration in China, contributing to 90.1 percent of
the rise in ozone-related mortality from 2013 to 2019. The problem has
persisted, with the average ozone concentration in monitored cities
reaching 145 Î¼g/mÂ³ in 2022, up by 5.8 percent from 2021.

While unfavorable weather conditions may play a role, there is clearly a
diminishing returns problem in the authoritarian approach to pollution
control. Since 2018, the annual reduction in PM2.5 levels has slowed.
There was no change in the average annual PM2.5 levels from 2018 to
2019, and only a slight decrease during 2020-22, largely due to the
reduced economic activities. The diminishing returns problem became more
apparent in 2023. Among the provincial capital cities in China, nearly
50 percent that had PM2.5 levels above the national standard of 35 Î¼g/mÂ³
showed improved air quality compared to the previous year. In contrast,
every city that met the national standard experienced a rise in PM2.5
concentrations.

[[A **major concern** is]{.mark} the increasing dependency on **[fossil

returned to the 2017 level of 60 percent. In the last quarter of 2023,

to 1,584 TWh, [[accounting for]{.mark} nearly [**70 percent** of]{.mark}
the nation's [electricity]{.mark} generation]{.underline}. This
escalating reliance on fossil fuels significantly contributes to air

standards, coal]{.mark} production and thermal power generation
**[increased]{.mark}** by 4.4 percent and 4.3 percent]{.underline},
respectively, compared to the previous year. The [[growing

**Li 23** \[Alexander F. Li (Schwarzman College, Tsinghua University),
Chen-Fei Qu (Institute of Energy, Environment, and Economy, Tsinghua
University), and Xi-Liang Zhang (Institute of Energy, Environment, and
Economy, Tsinghua University), February 2023, \"Exploring U.S.--China
climate cooperation through linked carbon markets,\" Advances in Climate
Change Research, Vol. 14, Issue 1, pp. 145-155,
https://doi.org/10.1016/j.accre.2023.01.005, njn; accessed 6-27-2025\]
**\*Note: MtCO2 expanded to "Metric tons CO2" in brackets for
readability**

1\. Introduction

To avert the worst effects of climate change, the world needs to rapidly
reduce carbon emissions. Emissions trading systems ([ETSs]{.underline})
[are a **cost-effective** policy tool for incentivizing
**decarbonization** that have been **widely discussed** and
implemented]{.underline} (Newell et al., 2014). There are over 20 ETSs
operating worldwide, covering over half of global GDP (ICAP, 2022). ETSs
are also under active development for fast-growing economies such as
those in Southeast Asia. [[**Coinciding** with]{.mark} the **increasing
[adoption]{.mark}** [of ETSs has been a push for **international
cooperation**]{.mark} in addressing climate change]{.underline}. In
2015, the Paris Climate Agreement featured 192 countries stating goals
to reduce carbon emissions in their Nationally Determined Contributions
(NDC). More recently at the 2021 UN Climate Change Conference in Glasgow
(COP26), [[these]{.mark} same [countries **promoted ETSs** and]{.mark}
the **[international linkage]{.mark}** of ETSs as an **avenue** [to
achieve]{.mark} **global [climate goals]{.mark}**]{.underline} through
the ratification of Article 6 of the Paris Agreement. International ETS
linkage stands as a promising opportunity to foster global cooperation
and accelerate the reduction of greenhouse gas emissions.

It is well described that [international emissions trading is a
**cost-effective** way to **reduce** global greenhouse gas
**emissions**]{.underline} (Carbone et al., 2009; Fujimori et al., 2015;
Fujimori et al., 2016; Zhang et al., 2017). ETS linkage takes advantage
of the differences in the marginal cost of abatement across regions and
allows market forces to allocate capital towards the lowest-cost methods
for emissions reduction (BÃ¶hringer and Welsch, 2004). Qi and Weng (2016)
uses a computable general equilibrium (CGE) model to analyze the impact
of multilateral ETS linkage on major economies such as the U.S., EU,
Japan, China, Brazil, and India and finds that all participating nations
have greater GDP in the linked ETS scenario compared to a scenario
without ETS linkage.

However, studies have also found that multilateral ETS linkage can
result in heterogeneous economic impacts with permit-exporting countries
experiencing slight income reduction (Babiker et al., 2004; Fujimori et
al., 2016; Zhang et al., 2017). This is largely due to terms-of-trade
effects, which refer to effects on the competitiveness of companies
operating within a linked ETS compared to an independent ETS (Gavard et
al., 2016). The carbon price for a linked ETS will reach an equilibrium
between the carbon prices of the independent regions. For carbon
allowance exporting regions, the linked ETS results in a higher carbon
price leading to higher costs for companies participating in the ETS,
especially in carbon-intensive industries such as manufacturing. This
higher cost reduces their competitiveness in a global market where
competitors may not be subject to the same cost increase (Winkler et
al., 2021). The effects of a carbon price on trade and competitiveness
makes ETS linkage a complex decision for which policymakers will need to
weigh economic and political implications (Flachsland et al., 2011).
Further, Pang et al. (2015) describes many of the practical obstacles
facing carbon market linkage, including overcoming coordination
challenges across three categories including: mutual recognition of
allowance allocation methods, identical compliance and banking
mechanisms, and coordinated monitoring, reporting, and verification
(MRV) standards. Due to these hurdles, some researchers believe that
broad multilateral ETSs would be difficult to implement (Tuerk et al.,
2011). Rather, [smaller-scale ETS linkages with fewer participants could
be **more likely** to emerge in the **near term**]{.underline} (Behr et
al., 2009).

[[One **partnership**]{.mark} that would be [**key** for]{.mark} global
[**climate success** is]{.mark}]{.underline} that [between [**China**
and the **U.S.**]{.mark}]{.underline}, the top two carbon-emitting
nations which together account for over 40% of annual global CO2
emissions (IEA, 2021). As part of the Paris Agreement, the U.S. has set
an NDC to reduce greenhouse gas emissions 50%--52% below 2005 levels by
2030. China has made commitments to peak carbon emissions by 2030 and
achieve carbon neutrality by 2060 (UNFCCC, 2022). The relationship
between the two nations has vacillated over time due to various
political and economic factors. However, [[the past **decade**]{.mark}
has [indicated]{.mark} that [**climate change** is an area both]{.mark}
nations [can]{.mark} return to [find **cooperation**]{.mark}
opportunities]{.underline} (USDS, 2021), [[and **commitments**]{.mark}
made by the U.S. and China [to **support**]{.mark} international
**[climate action]{.mark}**]{.underline} indicate this area can continue
to [[serve as **common ground**]{.underline}]{.mark}. China\'s national
ETS officially began operations in 2021. This ETS will cover emissions
from China\'s energy sector in its initial phase of operation, which
alone accounts for over 10% of global CO2 emissions (Pizer and Zhang,
2018). The U.S. has not implemented a national ETS, but regional
emissions trading systems cover major economic zones in California and
the Northeast (Narassimhan et al., 2018).

Several studies have investigated linkage between China\'s ETS and one
or two other ETSs, mostly focused on Europe or Asia. Li et al. (2019)
adopts a CGE model to show that ETS linkage promotes emission reductions
and improves economic welfare in both regions when compared to a
scenario without linkage. Winkler et al. (2021) and HÃ¼bler et al. (2014)
examine EU-China ETS linkage and identifies that while the EU benefits
from unrestricted trading, China\'s economy benefits the most when there
are restrictions to allowance trading. Several studies have modeled ETS
linkage among nations in Asia (Ma et al., 2019; Massetti and Tavoni,
2012; Li and Duan, 2021) and have found that participants, including
China as a permit-exporter, experience positive GDP or welfare effects
compared to an independent ETS scenario without linkage.

Overall, the literature shows that the net economic effect of ETS
linkage on China\'s economy depends on the structure and participants of
the scheme. However, there is limited study of U.S.--China linkage.
Gavard et al. (2016) and Gavard et al. (2011) explores U.S.--China ETS
linkage with varying restrictions on trading. Qi and Weng (2016) and
Fujimori et al. (2016) include the U.S., China, and other large
economies in their multilateral-linked ETS. However, these studies do
not include NDC updates made by nations in 2020 and 2021, including
strengthened emission reduction commitments by the U.S. Further, studies
typically consider scenarios with either bilateral ETS linkage or
multilateral ETS linkage, rarely comparing these forms of linkage.

This study takes a unique approach to ETS linkage by exploring scenarios
with both bilateral U.S.--China ETS linkage and multilateral linkage
with the U.S.--China and nations in Southeast Asia. Multilateral ETS
linkages are explored as the current U.S.--China bilateral relationship
is strained, and multilateral institutions have been shown to serve as
an effective method to bring adverse nations together to collaborate on
common challenges (Singh, 2011). In considering third parties to include
in an ETS with China and the U.S., we considered regions where both
nations engage heavily in trade and regions actively developing carbon
markets. Southeast Asia stood out as an important yet understudied
region. The Association of Southeast Asian Nations (ASEAN) is a top five
trading partner for both the U.S. and China, with the U.S. and China
trading over 300 billion and 500 billion USD in goods and services with
ASEAN respectively (World Bank, 2019). ASEAN has been projected to have
the fourth-largest GDP in the world by 2030, trailing the U.S., China
and the EU (GCABC, 2017). Along with this economic growth is a
forecasted rise in carbon emissions. Without emission reduction
policies, annual greenhouse gas emissions from ASEAN could double by
2040 to reach 2.3 Gt (Sandu et al., 2019). At the same time, Southeast
Asian nations have introduced various climate policies including Paris
Agreement NDCs and carbon markets. Worldwide 40% of the national ETSs
under development are in Southeast Asia, including Indonesia and
Vietnam, while other nations such as Malaysia, Philippines, and Thailand
are actively considering ETSs (ICAP, 2022). Despite this prominence in
global trade and climate, there is a gap in the literature examining
Southeast Asia\'s role in international emissions trading. Other major
regions with active carbon market development such as the EU were also
considered for linkage, but the EU has already been included in many ETS
linkage studies with China (Gavard et al., 2016; Li et al., 2019;
Winkler et al., 2021). This study compares bilateral with multilateral
ETS linkage as a form of U.S.--China climate cooperation, and analyzes
Southeast Asia as a partner for international emission trading.

We leveraged a CGE model to understand the effects of varying ETS
linkage scenarios on carbon emissions, energy use, and economic output.
Scenarios include no linkage, bilateral U.S.--China linkage, and
multilateral linkage between the U.S., China, and nations in Southeast
Asia. The particular CGE model used in this study is the China-in-Global
Energy Model. We have updated this model from previous studies to
reflect the latest technology developments and climate goals set by
governments. In providing this analysis, [[we]{.mark} offer an updated
**[assess]{.mark}**ment of **[emissions trading]{.mark}** as an avenue
[for **U.S.--China climate cooperation**]{.mark}.]{.underline}

\<\<TEXT CONDENSED, NONE OMITTED\>\>

2\. Methodology 2.1. The C-GEM model This study uses the China-in-Global
Energy Model (C-GEM), a multi-region, multi-sector, recursive-dynamic,
CGE model of the global economy. It has been developed by the Tsinghua
Institute of Energy, Environment, and Economy and the MIT Joint Program
on the Science and Policy of Global Change, and was first described in
detail in Qi et al. (2014). This model has been used in previous studies
on the effects of climate policies and technologies in China and
globally (Huang et al., 2020; Li and Duan., 2021; Qi and Weng, 2016;
Zhang et al., 2017). As in many CGE models, C-GEM aggregates consumers
and producers within each sector of the economy into a representative
consumer and representative producer. The representative consumer seeks
to maximize their own welfare by providing labor and capital, purchasing
goods and services, or making investments while subject to budget
constraints. The representative producer seeks to maximize their own
profits. The government collects taxes from consumers and producers
while also making financial transfers with households. C-GEM is
developed using the Mathematical Programming Subsystem for General
Equilibrium (MPSGE) and the Generalized Algebraic Modeling System (GAMS)
mathematical modeling language (Rutherford, 1999). It is solved as a
mixed complementarity problem and uses the PATH solver (Dirkse and
Ferris, 1995). 2.1.1. Regions and sectors C-GEM divides the global
economy into 17 geographic regions. Large economies are modeled as
individual regions (e.g. China, U.S., EU, Brazil) while smaller
economies are modeled as composite regions (e.g. Southeast Asia, Latin
America). Economic activities in each region are aggregated into the 19
production sectors and 3 final demand sectors shown in Table 1. The
electricity sector is also further broken across different generation
technologies (e.g. coal, natural gas, solar, wind, etc.). Table 1.
Sectors in the CGEM. \<\<TABLE OMITTED\>\> 2.1.2. Static module Overall,
C-GEM is organized into two modules: a static module and a dynamic
module. The static module describes economic activities such as
production, consumption, trade, and carbon emissions in a single time
period. The model solves the equilibrium in a given period, then updates
parameters according to the dynamic module before moving to the next
period. The static module primarily consists of four components: 1)
production and consumption, 2) international trade, 3) emissions, and 4)
closure mechanisms. The core mechanism to model each production sector
in C-GEM is a nested constant elasticity of substitution (CES) function.
Different nesting structures and elasticities are used to describe
different characteristics across industries. As an energy economic
model, C-GEM models both the values and quantities of energy. Carbon
emissions are calculated by applying constant emission factors to the
quantity of energy consumed. These factors are assumed to remain
constant across regions and over time. As climate policies such as ETSs
are enacted, the elasticities describe the willingness of producers to
substitute traditional fuels for lower-carbon fuels and reduce energy
consumption. C-GEM production sectors can reduce emissions through three
methods: 1) decreasing output and therefore reducing energy consumption,
2) improving energy efficiency, and 3) decreasing emissions per unit
energy consumption by adopting renewables or other low-carbon fuels.
Trade in C-GEM follows the Armington assumption (Armington, 1969). For
consumers and intermediate use, all goods except crude oil are treated
as Armington goods. This means consumers consider domestic goods and
imported goods as imperfect substitutes and purchases by consumers are a
(CES) composite of domestic and imported goods. Imported goods are also
a CES composite from different geographic regions in the model. 2.1.3.
Dynamic module The static module of the C-GEM serves as the foundation
for a recursive-dynamic model that forecasts the impact of policies on
global economies. The dynamic model operates on five-year period
increments, solving each period sequentially. 'Recursive dynamic'
indicates that each region makes decisions based on available
information for the current period rather than maximizing their utility
over the future. In general, there are three categories of parameters:
factor endowment change, technology change, and economic structure
change. Factor endowments are resources available to a region for
economic activity including effective labor, capital, and natural
resources. Capital growth of each region is determined by the investment
from the last period. The effective labor growth is calibrated so that
the baseline GDP matches historical trends. The endowment of land and
renewable resources are assumed constant. Technology change considers
three developments around energy. First, energy efficiency is modeled
through Autonomous Energy Efficiency Improvement (AEEI) parameters that
project improvements in energy used per unit of output across sectors.
C-GEM uses different AEEI values across regions as suggested by Webster
et al. (2008). Second, cost declines of fossil fuel substitute
technology are adopted from the International Renewable Energy
Association\'s power generation report (IRENA, 2021) and the Global EV
Outlook 2022 (IEA, 2022). C-GEM considers 11 technologies to be
substitutes for fossil fuels, of which eight are expected to be used at
notable scale by 2030 and are presented in Table 2. These include
low-carbon electricity generation (e.g. wind, solar, hydropower) as well
as alternative fuels. Table 2. The eight fossil fuel substitutes
considered at notable scale in 2030. \<\<TABLE OMITTED\>\> 2.1.4. Model
updates and calibration C-GEM is calibrated with the most recent
economic and energy data for China and global regions. Trade data is
sourced from version ten of the Global Trade Analysis Project (GTAP10)
database, which is the newest available version (Aguiar et al., 2019).
Energy and economic data from all countries are sourced from The World
Bank (TWB, 2021) and the International Energy Agency (IEA), with
additional data for China sourced from the National Bureau of Statistics
of China. Population growth, as a factor for GDP growth, is calibrated
such that GDP from 2020 to 2030 matches historical trends. The final
demand structure is calibrated to match historical shifts in economic
structure (primary, secondary, tertiary) across all nations and regions.
The emissions before the introduction of emission trading schemes are
calibrated according to the emissions projection in the STEPS scenarios
of World Energy Outlook (2021) (IEA, 2021). We used national and
regional emission trading schemes to simulate the impacts of current
policies in all regions of the model. Emission caps are set based on
NDCs. The model is calibrated to shifts in the economic structure of
regions over time (shifting from an agriculture and industrial economy
to a service economy) by adjusting the final demand structure. This is
done with a multiplier in the final demand for service sectors, which is
calibrated to match historical trends in the shift towards service
economies across regions. Specifically, the household\'s consumption
will use an increasingly larger proportion of service goods and a
smaller proportion of industrial and agricultural goods. In the model,
we scaled up the share of service goods in final consumption so that the
resulting mix of agricultural, industrial, and service sectors in each
region\'s economy in 2030 matches trends based on historical data from
The World Bank (TWB, 2019). We assumed that between 2020 and 2030, the
service sector as a percent of GDP grows by 0.75 percentage points in
the U.S., 7.5 percentage points in China, and 3.3 percentage points in
Southeast Asia. Further, compared to the previous version of C-GEM used
in 2020 to study China\'s domestic decarbonization (Huang et al., 2020),
the model version used in this study includes several updates that are
summarized in Table 3. Table 3. C-GEM updates. \<\<TABLE OMITTED\>\>
2.2. Scenarios This study explores the impacts of ETS linkage in 2030
across three different scenarios: 1) independent ETSs without linkage,
2) bilateral U.S.--China linkage, and 3) multilateral linkage between
China, the U.S. and nations in Southeast Asia. Across scenarios, nations
are assumed to successfully meet their 2030 NDCs. The independent ETS
scenario, named NOLINK, is one where the U.S. and China successfully
meet their NDCs through implementing national economy-wide ETSs without
international linkage. The CO2 emission cap for the U.S. follows its NDC
and is assumed to be 50% of CO2 emissions generated in 2005 (UNFCCC,
2022). The CO2 emissions cap for China is not set in the NDC. Instead,
we referenced estimates from the National Center for Climate Change
Strategy and International Cooperation which estimates emissions under
compliance with NDC commitments through a bottoms-up sector analysis
(NCSC, 2016). For Southeast Asia, which is modeled as a single region in
C-GEM, the CO2 emission cap is built bottoms up by aggregating the
individual emissions reduction goals from nations in Southeast Asia
(UNFCCC, 2022). For Malaysia, which is similar to China in setting
carbon intensity goals, we referred to literature estimates of
Malaysia\'s 2030 carbon emissions after meeting its NDC (Fulton et al.,
2017). The bilateral U.S.--China linkage scenario is named LINK. China
and the U.S. link their economy-wide ETSs with perfect market
assumptions. No trading restrictions are adopted on price or volume
quotas, and permits are allowed to freely trade with supply and demand.
The emission caps of the U.S. and China are the same as the NOLINK
scenario. The multilateral U.S.--China--Southeast Asia scenario is named
LINK+. C-GEM models all nations in Southeast Asia (Brunei, Cambodia,
Indonesia, Laos, Malaysia, Myanmar, Philippines, Singapore, Thailand,
Timor-Leste, Vietnam) as a single region. This modeled region is
collectively referred to as Southeast Asia (SEA). For the LINK+
scenario, the U.S., China, and Southeast Asia all participate in a fully
linked carbon market without trade restrictions. The emissions caps for
all regions are the same as the NOLINK scenario. 3. Results and
discussion 3.1. Carbon emissions across model scenarios The impact of
our modeling scenarios on carbon emissions and carbon prices is
summarized in Table 4 and Fig. 1. For the NOLINK scenario, China\'s
emissions are 10,500 MtCO2 in 2030, which is more than double the
combined emissions from the U.S. and Southeast Asia, 3125 MtCO2 and 2010
MtCO2 respectively. The simulated carbon price in the NOLINK scenario is
21.5 USD (tCO2)âˆ’1 for China, 134.0 USD (tCO2)âˆ’1 for the U.S., and 2.7
USD (tCO2)âˆ’1 for Southeast Asia. These carbon prices reflect different
marginal costs of abatement across regions, as well as the different
stringency of 2030 NDCs. Table 4. Summary of carbon emissions and
allowance trading across scenarios. \<\<TABLE OMITTED\>\> \<\<FIGURE
OMITTED\>\> Fig. 1. CO2 emissions compared across scenarios.

trading** to meet their carbon **emission caps**]{.underline}. Due to
its higher carbon price, [the U.S. is **incentivized** to **purchase**

In the LINK+ scenario, the U.S., China, and Southeast Asia all engage in
a joint ETS. With the highest independent carbon price, the U.S. remains
incentivized to import allowances from both China and Southeast Asia.
China is incentivized to import allowances from Southeast Asia. The
output is shown in Table 4 displays the net import and export activity
for each region. China remains a net carbon exporter of 802 MtCO2 of
allowances, 36% less than the quantity of allowances exported in the
LINK scenario. In total, China\'s domestic emissions are 9698 MtCO2
which represents a 7% decrease from the NOLINK scenario. Southeast Asia
exports 532 MtCO2 and its actual emissions are 1478 MtCO2. This is a 26%
reduction in emissions from the NOLINK scenario. The U.S. imports 1344
MtCO2 of allowances in the LINK+ scenario which is 7% more than the
quantity imported in the LINK scenario. Domestic emissions in the U.S.
are 43% higher than emissions in the NOLINK scenario. Through market
linkage, the carbon price equalizes to 27.8 USD (tCO2)âˆ’1.

3.2. Energy consumption across model scenarios

[The **[energy]{.mark}** industry [is the **primary source** of]{.mark}
CO2 [**emissions** in China]{.mark}]{.underline}, the U.S., and
Southeast Asia, [and will be **impacted** by any **ETS policy**
implementation]{.underline}. The carbon price created by the ETS policy
will change the price of different energy products for consumers,
thereby changing the overall quantity consumed. Fig. 2 summarizes the
primary energy consumption across regions and carbon market scenarios.
The historical energy consumption in 2020 for each region is also
included to serve as a historical reference.

\<\<FIGURE OMITTED\>\>

Fig. 2. Primary energy consumption in 2020 and across 2030 scenarios
(Lighter shading for 2020 indicates historical data).

Within the NOLINK scenario, we saw that an independent ETS in China
results in energy consumption totaling 3881 Mtoe. This represents a 12%
increase in energy consumption from the year 2020. [One **notable
change** in China\'s energy consumption is a **25% decrease** in coal
consumption compared to 2020]{.underline}. In contrast, [consumption of
**renewables** increases **sharply**, growing by 162%.]{.underline} In
the U.S., the NOLINK scenario results in total energy consumption of
1747 Mtoe, which represents a 19% decrease from 2020. The reduction in
CO2 emissions comes from a reduction in fossil fuel U.S.age, notably a
90% decrease in coal consumption. Similar to China, U.S. renewable
energy consumption more than doubles in the NOLINK scenario compared to
2020, growing 102%. This shows that creating economy-wide ETS in China
and the U.S. could help reduce coal consumption and stimulate the growth
of renewables.

[In the **LINK** scenario, [China\'s]{.mark} energy [**consumption**
totals]{.mark}]{.underline} 3538 Mtoe, which is 9% [[**lower**
than]{.mark} China\'s total energy consumption [in
NOLINK]{.mark}]{.underline}[. [The **biggest
reduction**]{.underline}]{.mark} [in energy **consumption** [comes from
**coal**]{.mark}, where consumption is **18% lower** compared
to]{.underline} the [NOLINK]{.underline} scenario. [[ETS linkage]{.mark}
also [**encourages** renewable]{.mark} energy
[development]{.mark}]{.underline}, though to a limited extent (2%
increase) compared with NOLINK. In the LINK scenario for the U.S., final
energy consumption increases to 2238 Mtoe. This is 28% higher than the
NOLINK scenario and even surpasses energy consumption in 2020.

\<\<TEXT CONDENSED, NONE OMITTED\>\>

In the LINK+ scenario, the U.S. maintains a similar energy consumption
structure to the bilateral linkage scenario. China, which exports fewer
carbon allowances in LINK+ compared to LINK, increases energy
consumption by 3% overall. The final energy consumption for Southeast
Asia in LINK+ is 676 Mtoe, which is 17% lower than the energy
consumption in the NOLINK scenario. Southeast Asia achieves this energy
consumption reduction by reducing coal consumption 54% compared to
consumption in 2020. 3.3. Economic output across model scenarios The
financial transfers and changes to carbon price resulting from ETS
linkage will affect production output for all covered sectors of the
economy and therefore GDP. Fig. 3 shows the change in GDP between the
linked ETS scenarios and the NOLINK independent ETS scenario. The
overarching observation is that the total GDP of all ETS participants in
the LINK and LINK+ scenarios surpasses GDP in the NOLINK scenario. This
supports the principle that carbon markets are a cost-effective tool to
facilitate decarbonization. However, Fig. 3 also reveals variations in
GDP impact across regions. \<\<FIGURE OMITTED\>\> Fig. 3. GDP change
compared to the NOLINK scenario (% GDP). The LINK scenario shows that
China\'s GDP is negatively impacted while the U.S. GDP is positively
impacted by ETS linkage. For China, bilateral ETS linkage with the U.S.
will result in 0.19% GDP decrease compared to the NOLINK scenario. This
reduction in GDP is due to strong terms-of-trade effects resulting from
the rise in carbon price from 21.5 USD in NOLINK to 31.6 USD in LINK, an
increase of 47%. This increased carbon price serves as an increase in
cost for the entire economy and results in reduced output across
sectors, specifically carbon-intensive sectors that engage in export
activity. The revenue China gains from selling carbon offsets to the
U.S. does not fully compensate for this reduction in output. In
contrast, the U.S. GDP in the bilateral linkage scenario is 0.71%
greater than NOLINK. Carbon market linkage provides the U.S. a
lower-cost path to achieving its carbon emission goals. One key
observation is that the LINK+ scenario results in better GDP outcomes
for China and the U.S. compared to the LINK scenario, while also
delivering positive GDP outcomes for Southeast Asia. For China, the
reduction in GDP is limited to 0.13% compared to the 0.19% decrease in
the bilateral linked ETS. The U.S. sees a slight further increase in GDP
from 0.71% in LINK to 0.74% in LINK+. Southeast Asia experiences a
slight increase in GDP of 0.05% compared to the independent carbon
market. Incorporating Southeast Asia into the carbon market reduces the
linked ETS carbon price from 31.6 USD in LINK to 27.8 USD in the LINK+
scenario. This reduction in carbon price serves as a reduced cost burden
for the U.S. and China to meet their economic goals. For China, the
LINK+ carbon price is only 29% higher than its independent ETS carbon
price. For the U.S., it is nearly an 80% decrease in the carbon price.
To further investigate the economic effects of ETS linkage, Fig. 4
displays the sectoral effects of the LINK+ scenario. For China and
Southeast Asia, a higher carbon price provides more incentive to
transition away from carbon-intensive industries. In China, there are
reductions in output from the transportation, chemical manufacturing,
coal mining, and coal electricity generation sectors totaling 19 billion
USD relative to the NOLINK scenario. Fig. 4 shows that Southeast Asia
has notable reductions in output from the same sectors as China,
totaling 22 billion USD. This indicates that the transportation,
chemical manufacturing, and coal-based energy sectors see the largest
reduction in output due to increased carbon prices. Other
carbon-intensive sectors such as mining, oil refining, and metal
manufacturing also have reduced output in China and Southeast Asia,
though to a lesser extent. On the other hand, sectors with lower carbon
intensity are likely to increase their output relative to the NOLINK
scenario. China increases output in its services, construction, and
natural gas electricity sectors totaling 11 billion USD. Southeast Asia
increases output in the same sectors totaling 14 billion USD. It is
notable that while China\'s wind and solar electricity generation output
increases by only 1.5 billion USD, Southeast Asia\'s wind and solar
electricity generation increases by 5 billion USD. It is possible that
Southeast Asia\'s renewable energy sector benefits more from the
increased carbon price because it is starting from a less mature
position than China. Our model forecasts that China consumes 153 Mtoe of
renewable energy in 2020 whereas Southeast Asia only consumed 11 Mtoe.
Further, the change in carbon price for Southeast Asia, from 2.7 USD to
27.8 USD is a change of over 900% while the change for China from 21.5
USD to 27.8 USD is only a change of 30%. Fossil fuel energy players in
Southeast Asia face a drastically higher cost for carbon allowances,
opening the door for faster renewable energy adoption in the region.
\<\<FIGURE OMITTED\>\> Fig. 4. Sectoral value added, change between
LINK+ and NOLINK scenario (billion USD, âˆ— indicates values beyond axis).
U.S. economic sectors generally experience the opposite effect compared
to China and Southeast Asia. Lower costs for carbon allowances allow
carbon-intensive industries to increase their relative output. The
sectors with the largest increase in output for the U.S. are
transportation, chemical manufacturing, and natural gas production,
totaling 72 billion USD. Sectors with low carbon intensity decreased
output in LINK+ compared to NOLINK, with the services sector reducing
output by 40 billion USD. Notably, while the gas and coal electricity
generation sectors have higher output, U.S. wind and solar sectors
reduced output by a total of 14 billion USD. This indicates that the
multilateral ETS provides fewer incentives for U.S. renewables than a
domestic independent ETS.

4\. Discussion

At a high level, our [[emissions]{.mark} results [show **similar
trends** to other]{.mark} CGE [studies]{.mark} of carbon market
linkage]{.underline}. Li et al. (2019), Winkler et al. (2021), and
Gavard et al. (2016) all show that more developed nations such as the
U.S. or EU will have higher carbon prices than China. As a result,
[carbon [market linkage will **incentivize**]{.mark}]{.underline} these
[nations to rely on **importing** lower-cost **[carbon
allowances]{.mark}**]{.underline} over domestic emissions reduction [[to
meet **climate goals**]{.underline}]{.mark}. In the ETS linkage
scenarios, the U.S. consumes more fossil fuels, with coal consumption
over 300% greater and natural gas consumption 40% greater than the
NOLINK scenario. Further, these scenarios result in weaker incentives
for renewables, with the U.S. consuming about 13% less renewable
electricity than the NOLINK scenario across both LINK and LINK+. On the
other hand, [[China]{.mark} is **incentivized** to **[reduce]{.mark}**
its [total energy **consumption**]{.mark}, primarily by **reducing** the
usage of **coal** by 18%]{.underline}. This shows that U.S.--China ETS
linkage will have contrasting effects on energy consumption in the two
nations.

There is also notable contrast in the GDP impact of ETS linkage for
China and Southeast Asia in the LINK+ scenario. While both regions are
carbon exporters, Southeast Asia\'s GDP impact is positive while
China\'s GDP impact is negative compared to the NOLINK scenario.
Understanding this difference in outcome requires examining the two
primary economic effects resulting from ETS linkage. First, examining
income gained from selling carbon allowances reveals that China
generates 22.2 billion USD and Southeast Asia generates 14.8 billion
USD. This shows income benefits to China and Southeast Asia are in the
same order of magnitude. Second, examining the terms-of-trade effects
requires looking at exports from both regions. We model exports for
China as 1.2 trillion USD and for Southeast Asia as 135 billion USD in
the LINK+ scenario. China\'s exports are an order of magnitude greater
than that of Southeast Asia. This creates greater losses for China as
higher carbon prices will predominantly hurt the competitiveness of
export companies in a global market. In total then, China and Southeast
Asia receive positive benefits from ETS linkage on the same order of
magnitude, while negative terms-of-trade effects are felt more heavily
in China as its exports are an order of magnitude greater than Southeast
Asia.

Overall, [[ETS linkage **reduces** the]{.mark} net [economic
**burden**]{.mark} faced by nations to meet their **climate
goals**]{.underline}. This is evident in that the [**total GDP** for
participants]{.underline} in both LINK and LINK+ [is **higher** than the
total GDP in the NOLINK scenario]{.underline}. The total GDP for the
U.S. and China in the LINK scenario is 0.24% higher than NOLINK, while
the total GDP for all three participants in the LINK+ scenario is 0.27%
higher than NOLINK. The results from our C-GEM model indicate that the
multilateral scheme is more favorable for China and the U.S. than a
bilateral scheme, and more favorable to Southeast Asia than staying
independent.

These economic results vary slightly from bilateral and multilateral ETS
linkage results in existing literature. While our results show a
favorable economic impact for the U.S. under bilateral ETS linkage,
Gavard et al. (2016) shows that bilateral U.S.--China ETS linkage leads
to net negative economic impacts in both countries. This discrepancy may
be attributed to Gavard et al. (2016) modeling an ETS only covering
energy-intensive industries as opposed to an economy-wide ETS. Further,
in the multilateral scenario, our model shows a negative GDP impact for
China and a positive GDP impact for Southeast Asia despite both
exporting carbon allowances. This output is similar to Fujimori et al.
(2016), which also shows differing economic impacts for permit
exporters, but differs from Qi and Weng (2016), which shows that all
nations participating in a multilateral ETS have positive GDP impacts.

Several limitations in this analysis deserve acknowledgment. First, it
is limited in the scope of ETS linkage scenarios explored. We primarily
focused on national economy-wide ETS linkage without considering
subnational ETS linkages. The study also assumes that nations are
capable of achieving their NDC emission targets and that these targets
would serve as carbon caps for nations across linkage scenarios.
Scenarios where nations fail to meet NDC goals were not considered.
Further, the results of CGE models, such as the one used, rely on a
large number of parameters. Constraints on accessible data can affect
the accuracy of chosen parameters and model results. The CGE model also
assumes perfectly competitive markets and rational consumers, which may
not be representative of all real-world situations. Despite these
limitations, the study still provides a useful framework for analyzing
the potential impacts of ETS linkage. It offers insights on the
contrasting emissions and energy consumption effects of ETS linkage
between the U.S. and China, and highlights the advantages of
multilateral ETS linkage that includes nations in Southeast Asia.

5\. Conclusion

[**[Emissions trading]{.mark}** systems [are]{.mark} a policy mechanism
[**increasingly** being **adopted**]{.mark} by China, the
U.S.]{.underline}, and nations in Southeast Asia [[to
**decarbonize**]{.mark} their economies and meet their **climate
goals**]{.underline}. Were these regions to expand their emissions
trading schemes into national economy-wide systems and meet their 2030
NDCs, the resulting carbon price would be 21.5 USD (tCO2)âˆ’1 in China,
134 USD (tCO2)âˆ’1 in the U.S., and 2.7 USD (tCO2)âˆ’1 in Southeast Asia.
This study considers connecting these national schemes in either a
bilateral U.S.--China ETS or multilateral U.S.--China--Southeast Asia
ETS and compares the emissions, energy, and economic effects of linkage.
As the region with the highest carbon price, the U.S. imports about 1300
MtCO2 across scenarios. [China and Southeast Asia engage in **further
decarbonization** in order to have excess allowances for export,
**reducing** emissions below their NDCs]{.underline}. The energy
consumption across regions reflects these changes in emissions, with
China and Southeast Asia reducing energy consumption and increasing
renewable energy usage while the U.S. increases energy consumption and
relies more on coal and natural gas.

Comparing across scenarios particularly highlights the economic
advantage of multilateral ETS linkage relative to bilateral ETS linkage.
In the bilateral linkage scenario, the U.S. experiences a net GDP gain
of 0.71% while China experiences a net GDP loss of 0.19% compared to the
independent ETS scenario. Negative economic effects stem from
terms-of-trade effects, where China\'s exporting companies are
disadvantaged in a global market due to higher carbon prices. Adding
Southeast Asia to establish a multilateral linked ETS lowers the linked
carbon price and minimizes China\'s GDP losses from 0.19% to 0.13%. At
the same time, this multilateral linkage scenario leads to relative GDP
gain for both the U.S. and Southeast Asia.

[As **policymakers** from China and the U.S. **explore options** for
climate cooperation, linked ETSs **warrant consideration**]{.underline}.
Our study offers two key implications. First, we show the differentiated
effects of ETS linkage on each country. For the U.S., ETS linkage
provides a path to meet its climate goals that bolster its GDP relative
to a scenario without linkage. [[For China, ETS linkage
**promotes**]{.mark} domestic **[cleantech]{.mark}** industries
[and]{.mark} accelerates domestic
**[decarbonization]{.mark}**]{.underline}. [ETS [linkage]{.mark} can
[become]{.mark} more **politically [feasible]{.mark}** [should
these]{.mark} benefits **[align]{.mark}** with **[national
priorities]{.mark}**]{.underline}. Second, our study highlights the
positive benefits of including Southeast Asia in the linked ETS,
resulting in more favorable GDP outcomes for all participants.
Multilateral ETS linkage should be explored as an avenue that may be
more economically favorable and politically feasible.

Ultimately, this study serves to provide illustrative cases of
[U.S.--China [ETS **linkage**]{.mark} that [can **guide climate
action**]{.mark} plans and research]{.underline}. Future studies can
explore smaller-scale cooperation including state/province linkage,
indirect linkage, or restricted emissions trading. Additionally,
multilateral linkage with other regions such as South Korea, Japan, or
the EU can be considered. Future work will also be needed as nations
progressively update their climate commitments and new climate
technology is deployed.

**China [says yes]{.underline}\-\--pollution control is an area of
[mutual agreement]{.underline}.**

Researchers at RAND and the Swedish Defence Research Agency looked at
where China is operating in the Arctic, what it wants, and what that
could mean for regional security. They concluded that [**[China
has]{.mark}** made only limited inroads in the Arctic, but that\'s not
for lack of trying.]{.underline} "The threat should not be inflated,"
said Stephanie Pezard, a senior political scientist at RAND who
specializes in Arctic security. "But at the same time, [they have **[a
clear intent to not be excluded from Arctic]{.mark}** developments as
the region becomes more accessible.]{.underline} The real questions are,
How much of a role do they want, and [what does that mean for an Arctic
nation like the United States?"]{.underline} Conditions in the Arctic
have always been so extreme, the distances so vast, that even rivals
like the United States and Russia were forced to cooperate there. But
the Arctic is warming faster than anywhere else on the planet. Sea
routes that sailors and explorers have dreamed about for centuries are
starting to open. [The promise of Arctic riches---oil, minerals, trade
routes, even fish---has started to draw interest from far outside the
northern latitudes. China has declared itself a "near-Arctic state," a
designation it invented to push for a greater role in Arctic
governance]{.underline}. It has dispatched research expeditions, sought
to establish mining and gas operations, and envisioned a network of
shipping routes crossing the Arctic, a "silk road on ice." It describes
itself as an "active participant, builder, and contributor in Arctic
affairs," one that has "spared no efforts to contribute its wisdom to
the development of the Arctic region." But in the Arctic, as in the rest
of the world, the United States sees China as a potentially
destabilizing force, with the economic and military power to try to bend
the established order to its liking. The Pentagon considers China its
"pacing challenge" of the foreseeable future. Its Arctic strategy,
released in October, pays particular attention to the risk of China
using commercial or scientific access to the Arctic for military
advantage. Researchers at RAND set out to document known Chinese
activities in the North American Arctic, which touches Alaska, Canada,
and Greenland. Their counterparts in Sweden focused on the European
side, from Iceland, through the Nordic countries, to Russia. What they
found: Especially in the North American Arctic, "there\'s not a ton
going on," Pezard said. China has invested in a handful of mining
operations, mostly chasing valuable rare earth minerals. It has trade
partnerships with Greenland and a small stake in a zinc mine in Alaska.
A Chinese company tried to buy a shuttered U.S. Navy base in Greenland,
but the Danish government quashed the idea. China has used loans and
infrastructure deals to prop open the door for itself in countries
across Asia, Africa, and Latin America. But the countries of the North
American Arctic have generally taken a hard look at any proposed Chinese
investments---and often a hard pass. Canada blocked a \$150 million gold
mine deal that would have put Chinese interests too close to military
installations. Greenland has held up plans for another Chinese mine over
concerns about pollution. "We didn\'t see the more nefarious activities
that we\'ve seen in other parts of the world, like predatory lending or
influence over local decisions, that are more damaging to international
norms," said Stephen Flanagan, an adjunct senior fellow at RAND and
former senior director for defense policy and strategy at the U.S.
National Security Council. "All of the governments are being careful in
how they deal with China. It\'s a \'buyer beware\' approach." RAND\'s
study did not include the Russian side of the Arctic. That may be
China\'s best route to greater Arctic influence. The Arctic Council, a
governing body of Arctic States and indigenous nations, suspended its
meetings last year, refusing to engage with Russia after it attacked
Ukraine. A panel of experts that RAND convened for its study noted that
Russia could seek to form its own Arctic governing council, with a more
central role for its ally, China. The experts thought that was possible,
but not necessarily probable. Despite its joint operation in the Bering
Sea this past fall, and codevelopment of a natural gas project in the
Russian Arctic, Moscow has also been wary of letting China pursue its
ambitions so close to its home shores. And rather than walk away from
the existing council, Russia has called for it to resume its meetings,
with Russia back at the table. For now, the U.S. should continue to make
the Arctic a diplomatic, economic, and strategic priority, to
demonstrate its commitment to the region and its people, researchers
concluded. It should work to strengthen solidarity among its Arctic
allies---and explore conditions under which it could restart some
engagement with Russia, such as on search-and-rescue preparedness. But
the U.S. should also recognize that [**engaging with China** in the
**Arctic** does not have to be a **win-or-lose**
proposition]{.underline}. [[There are **opportunities** to
cooperate]{.underline}]{.mark}---[[on **climate change**]{.mark}, for
example, [or **pollution** control]{.mark}]{.underline}. [China has
already [played a **key role** in]{.mark} international [agreements to
**protect**]{.mark} **Arctic [fisheries]{.mark}**]{.underline} and to
create maritime shipping regulations. On paper, at least, [[both
**China** and the **U**]{.mark}nited **[S]{.mark}**tates [are
**committed** to ensuring the Arctic remains a **region of
peace**]{.mark} and stability]{.underline}. The next few years will be a
crucial test. If current projections hold, the Arctic could see its
first ice-free summer by 2030.

**Otherwise, unmitigated [pollution]{.underline} will
[destroy]{.underline} the [legitimacy]{.underline} of the Chinese
Communist Party. Declining legitimacy will provoke the CCP to launch
[diversionary wars]{.underline} of aggression.**

Neither report was necessarily wrong (Beijing's smog is always worse in

expansion and economic growth. [Air, water, and soil

Interestingly, while policy elites in Beijing and Washington debate
whether China's rise threatens international peace and security, they

**dissatisfaction** and **frustration** among the populace, a breeding
ground for **[mass protests]{.mark}** directed at the
state]{.underline}. In the post-Mao era, [[the]{.underline}]{.mark}
Chinese Communist Party (**[[CCP]{.underline}]{.mark}**) [[needs to
**continuously justify** its rule by]{.underline}]{.mark} not just
sustaining rapid growth but also [consistently accomplishing **[concrete
policy]{.mark}** goals]{.underline}. As their prosperity increases,
[[the Chinese people **increasingly value**]{.mark} good
**[health]{.mark}**]{.underline} and other things beyond basic earnings.
As a result, the gap between popular expectations and the state's
capacity to deliver promised gains only becomes wider. Under Xi Jinping,
the partystate dominance has reached its zenith in the post-Mao era, so
[[government **failure** to deliver clean **air**]{.mark}, safe water,
and]{.underline} untainted [food [may]{.mark} well [lead people to

but also [the **legitimacy** of [the]{.mark} **political [system
itself]{.mark}**. The]{.underline} lack of accountability and
